the  coUectlons  altogether  were  regarded  with  sometlolng  of  superstitlous

inteUIgent  interest  by  those  who  valued  them  chlefly  as  the  materlals 
of  future  hlstory.     The  records  of  the  Church  of  Glasgow,  the  foundation

It  is  impossible  to  pass  altogether  without  notice  the  person  who  first 
directed  attention  to  the  long  neglected  records  of  the  Church  of  Glasgow, 
and  to  whoin  it  is  probably  owing  that  they  have  been  preserved,  at  least 
in  the  complete  state  in  wliich  we  find  them.  A  fuUer  memoir  of  his  life 
and  learned  laboiu-s  is  to  be  desired  by  all  who  feel  any  interest  in  Scotch 
historyj  Thomas  Innes,  son  of  James  Innes,  wadsetter  of  Drumgask, 
and  descended  from  the  Inneses  of  Drainie,  was  born  at  Drumgask  about 
the  year  1G67.  His  family  had  always  been  Cathohcs,  and  he  was  sent 
to  the  Scots  College  of  Paris  in  1681,  and  received  the  tonsmre  in 
1684.  He  took  priesfs  ordei-s  in  1691.  In  1692  hls  attcntion  was 
turned  to  the  valuable  collections  in  the  Scots  College  and  the  Char- 
treuse  at  Paris ;  and  it  was  naturally  strongly  fixed  upon  the  records  of 
Glasgow,  by  his  discovery  in  them,  of  proofs  of  the  legitimacy  of  the 
royal  hne.  He  was  employed  as  a  parish  priest  in  Magny,  a  town  half- 
way  between  Paris  and  Rouen,  for  two  years,  but  again  returned  to  the 
College  in  1697.  In  the  spring  of  the  foUowing  year  he  went  to  Scotland 
on  the  Scotch  mission,  and  served  as  a  priest  in  the  parish  of  Inverawn,  in 
thc  diocese  of  Moray.  He  returned  in  the  year  1702  to  act  in  the  capacity 
oi  prcBjectus  studiorum  in  the  CoUegc,  and  as  agent  for  the  Scotch  mission  at 
Paris.  He  had  now  an  oj^portunity  of  completing  the  arrangement  of  the 
Charters  and  MSS.  in  the  custody  of  his  iiouse,  and  preparing  liis  great

When  the  French  Revolution  threatened  destruction  to  all  records,  and 
especially  those  of  monarchy  and  the  priesthood,  the  poor  brethren  of  the 
Scots  College  were  not  found  well  fitted  to  resist  the  storm."  Alexander 
Gordon,  who  was  then  principal,  escaped  from  France,  and  took  refuge  in 
Scotland.  The  other  members  of  the  College  were  scattered  in  different 
directions.  Alexander  Innes,  the  great-grandnephew  of  Father  Thomas 
Innes,  alone  remained  in  the  Scots  CoUege,  and  upon  him  fell  the  storm 
which  the  others  had  foreseen  and  escaped.  He  was  imprisoned  in  the  same 
prison  with  the  EngUsh  nuns,  and  he,  as  well  as  his  companions,  were  order- 
ed  for  execution,  and  wero  only  saved  by  the  catastrophc  of  Robespierre 
happening  on  the  very  day  appointed  for  their  death.  When  the  Abbe 
Paul  MTherson,  afterwards  the  venerable  rector  of  the  Scots  College  at

ever  seemed  most  valuable,  including  many  of  their  MSS.,  and  dispatched

peace  of  Utrecht,  and  by  Dr  Brown,  in  his 
Ilistory  of  the  Highlands.

"  On  Tuesday,  20th  November  1838,  an 
inquest  was  held  at  the  Blue  Anchor  Tavern, 
St  Mary-at-Hill,  Thames  Street,  London, 
on  Mr  Robert  Watson,  aged  88,  who  had

in  bed,  by  twisting  his  neckcloth  with  a  poker. 
He  had  arrived  in  that  tavern  in  March  frora 
Boulogne,  and  after  staying  five  weeks  went

on  the  intimate  friend  of  Horne  Tooke  up

toailey  for  conspiracy,  and  acquitted  ;  that,

terest  of  Lady  M'D.  in  a  Swedish  ship,  in- 
which  he  was  nearly  taken,  on  suspicion  of 
being  Thomas  Hardy.  He  went  afterwards 
to  Paris,  and  was  employed  by  Napoleon  to

avowed  Deist.  He  went  from  France  to

Having  mentioned  the  circumstances  under 
which  the  Jacobite  papers  of  Cardinal  York 
found  their  way  to  Englaud,  it  may  be  allow- 
able  to  add  some  details  given  Ijy  Abbe  Mac- 
pherson,  of  those  belonging  to  Prince  Charles 
Edward.  The  Prince  left  all  his  papers  to 
his  natural  daughter,  the  Ducliess  of  Albany, 
who  gave  them  in  charge  to  her  chaplain, 
Waters,  in  whose  custody  they  remained  after 
her  death,  with  the  sanction  of  the  Cardinal. 
Sir  Johu  Ilippesley  having  left  England  to 
avoid  Warren  Hastings'  trial,  was  in  Rome 


about  1794-5,  and,  having  soen  these  docu- 
ments  in  Waters's  posseasion,  he  wrote  to 
Burke,  who  mentioned  them  to  the  Prince  of 
Wales.  His  Royal  Highness,  feeling  a  warm 
interest  in  the  reeovery  of  the  papers,  autho- 
rized  Sir  John  to  treat  for  tlieirpurchase.  Aiiter 
some  correspondence,  Waters,  in  1798,  agreed 
to  give  them  up,  on  condition  of  receiving  a 
pension  of  £50  a-year,  which,  however,  he  did 
jot  live  to  draw,  having  died  in  1799.  The 
manuscripts  were  consigned  to  the  British 
Vice-Consul  at  Civita  Vecchia,  to  wait  the 
arrival  of  the  frigate  iii  which  they  were  to 
be  shipped  ;  but  that  port  having  fallen  into 
the  hands  of  the  French,  they  could  not  be 
moved.  The  Prince  being  very  anxious  for 
their  safety,  Signor  Bonelli,  an  Italian  gen- 
tleman,  then  resident  in  London,  who  was 
after  the  poace  Britisli  Vice-Consul  at  Rome, 
was  sent  out  by  the  British  Government  to 
attempt  their  recovery.  On  arriving  at  Rome, 
he  applied  for  assistance  to  Abbe  Macpher- 
son,  and  with  much  difficuUy  procured  a  pass- 
port  for  Ci\ita  Vecchia,  British  subjects  being 
then  jealously  prevented  by  the  French  from 
approaching  the  coast.  Having  aseertained 
from  the  Vice-Consul  where  the  papers  lay, 
he  requested  leave  from  the  French  com- 
mandant  of  the  place  to  search  among  them

happening  to  take  up  a  copy  of  James  II.'s  Me- 
nioirs,  pronounced,  that  as  thc  papors  scemed

XVI  PKEFACE. 

its  inaccuracy  ;  and,  so  far  as  it  contains  copies  from  the  existing  records, 
it  is  now  unimportant ;  but  in  it  we  liave  extracts  of  obits,  and  of  a  visita- 
tion  of  the  Cathedral,  which  are  not  to  be  found  elsewhere. 

From  all  these  materials,  I  have  endeavoured  to  arrange,  in  chronological 
order,  the  whole  extant  and  known  muniments  of  the  bishopric  of  Glasgow. 
I  believe  every  document  contained  in  any  of  these  registers  or  collections, 
will  be  found  in  the  present  work.  The  arrangement  in  order  of  time,  has 
only  been  departed  from  in  a  few  instances,  for  the  manifest  convenience  of 
placing  together  deeds  conccrning  the  same  subject,  not  very  distant  in  date. 
In  the  early  part  of  such  a  collection,  the  precise  date  of  deeds  is  necessai-Uy 
matter  of  conjecture.  In  defence  of  the  present  arrangement,  it  may  be 
sufRcient  to  say,  that  the  order  of  Father  Innes  was  rarely  departed  from 
but  upon  absolute  evidence,  arising  from  documents  to  which  he  had  not 
access.  The  deeds  from  original  charters,  and  from  the  two  ancient  regis- 
ters,  are  printed  Avith  the  abbreviations  found  in  their  originals.  Those  taken 
from  what  may  be  called  modern  transcripts,  that  is,  from  copies  of  the  last 
century,  are  printed  for  the  most  part  without  contractions.  Repetitions 
have  been  avoided  as  much  as  possible;  and  where  a  deed  that  had  afready 
been  printed  was  found  to  occur  unaltered  in  a  subsequent  article,  I 
have  thought  it  sufficient  to  describe  it  by  a  reference  to  its  number  as  first 
printed. 

The  contents  of  the  ancient  registers  are  throughout  printed  without 
abridgement.  The  zeal  of  the  collectors  of  the  great  French  transcript 
has  loaded  the  latter  part  of  it  with  an  immense  mass  of  documents,  chiefly 
recording  the  foundation  of  obits,  altarages,  chaplainries,  and  other  minor 
endowments  in  the  Cathedral,  which  are  here  given  at  length,  when  they 
are  connected  with  events  of  historical  interest,  or  when  their  terms,  or 
the  views  of  the  grantcr,  are  distinguished  from  the  usual  style.  But  the 
greater  part  are  in  these  respects  so  uniform,  and  they  unhickily  fell  upon 
an  age  of  such  verbosity,  that  it  was  considered  expedient  to  curtail  them.

elected  Bishop  in  1319,  and  Lindesay  succeedcd  him  in  1321.'

John  Cameron  succccded  hiin  in  the  bishoprick  as  well  as  in  thc  officc  of 
chanccllor,  after  thc  scc  had  been  vacant  for  a  year.  Hc  had  previously 
been  Secretaiy  of  State  and  provost  of  Lincluden.  He  continued  chancellor 
till  1440.  He  built  thc  "  great  tower  "  of  the  Bishops'  Palace  in  Glasgow, 
on  which  his  arms  were  to  bc  seen  in  the  last  century ;  and  also  thc 
Chapter-house,  begun  by  Bishop  Lauder.  He  has  been  accused  of  great 
avarice  and  oppression,  not  on  very  good  evidence.  Buchanan  relates  the 
manner  of  his  death,  (^which  took  place  at  Lochwood  on  Christmas-eve 
1447)  with  some  prodigious  circumstances,  representcd  as  a  judgment  on 
his  -wicked  life.' 

The  period  of  the  ncxt  reign  is  now  chiefly  interesting  to  us  as  giving 
birth  to  the  most  important  offspring  of  the  Episcopal  Church  of  Glasgow, 
its  Universitv.  It  was  constituted  by  a  bull  of  Pope  Nicholas  V.,  dated  on 
the  7th  of  the  ides  of  January  1450,  and  had  a  charter  of  privileges  and 
exemptions  from  the  King,  and  another  from  the  Bishop  and  Chapter,  1453.' 

The  general  jubilee  proclaimed  in  1450  on  the  termination  of  the  great 
papal  scliism  was  extended  to  Scotland,  andpenitential  visits  and  ofFerings  at 
the  Cathedral  of  Glasgow  declared  equally  meritorious  with  those  at  Rome  ; 
the  offerings  on  the  occasion  being  distributed,  one  third  to  the  fabric  of  the 
church  of  Glasgow,  one  to  other  pious  uses  in  Scotland,  and  a  tliird  to 
Rome."  An  indulgence  with  regard  to  Lent,  and  a  royal  concession  that 
bishops  might  make  testamcnts,"  are  common  to  all  Scotland,  and  very  well 
known.  A  new  protection  to  the  burgh,  and  an  extensive  grant  of  regality 
to  the  bishop,^  mark  the  greatness  of  his  influence.

James  Bethune,  Bishop  elect  of  Galloway,  was  postulated  to  the  see  of  Bishops. 
Glasgow,  9th  November  1508,  and  consecrated  on  the  15th  April  1509, 
at  Stirhng.  He  was  previously  Lord  Treasurer,  but  resigned  that  office 
on  his  being  preferred  to  the  archbishoprick.  He  held  other  great  church 
benefices,  as  the  abbacies  of  Arbroath  and  KiKvinning.  He  was  made 
chancellor  of  the  kingdom  in  1515,  and  took  a  leading  part  in  the  poUtics  of 
the  time  against  the  party  of  the  Douglases.  In  1523  he  was  translated 
to  the  see  of  St  Andrews. 

The  chief  proceedings  recorded  in  the  reign  of  James  V.  are  connected  r.  jac.  V. 
with  the  claim  of  the  archbishops  of  Glasgow  to  independence,  and  the 
assumption  of  superiority  by  the  Archbishop  of  St  Anch-ews  as  primate," 
a  dispute  which  gave  rise  to  the  most  unseemly  proceedings  at  home,  and 
contentions  and  pleas  in  the  court  of  Rome  "  of  the  quhilkis  pleyis,"  in  the 
words  of  parliament,  "  the  expensis  is  unestimable  dampnage  to  the  realme."'' 

Thc  formula  of  the  oath  of  obedience  by  a  suffragan  to  his  metropolitan 
is  not  without  interest.*^

bishop  of  Glasgow,  and  consecrated  at  Edinburgh  on  the  5th  of  February 
1525.  He  was  appointed  chancellor  of  the  kingdom,  21st  August  152S, 
which  office  he  held  till  1543,  and  died  in  April  1547.  His  character  and 
the  transactions  of  his  life  are  matter  of  histor)'.     If  he  has  been  roughly

MuLTAS  quidem  perlustravi  regiones,  earundem  mores  et  cleri  plebisque  devotiones

sumpto  calamo,  ad  honorem  sanctissimi  confessoris  atque  pontificis  Kentegemi  qui

prout  potui  devote  composui.

Ixxxli  APPENDIX  II. 

Omni  namque  tempore  menstruo,  dissolutis  naturaliter  membrorum  compagibus  tam 
in  virglnis  quam  in  femine  janua  patulus  patet  semper  introitus  .  Et  quia  hic  latuit 
juvenem,  adeo  seductus  abibat,  ut  vicem  despectionis  quam  dudum  a  virgine 
passus  est,  in  illa  retortam  letus  reliquit  .  Quod  ergo  factum  pro  nichilo  deputatum 
illi,  donec  per  sanctum  Kentegemum  filium  suum  tempore  longo  elapso  ad  memo- 
riam  revocasset,  ut  in  sequentibus  scriptum  est  .  Virgo  autem  in  pristiua  voluntate 
perseverans,  nolebat  cuiquam  rei  eventum  propalare  .  Crescente  itaque  iu  utero 
prole,  et  matre  nihilominus  hoc  silente,  omnibus  demum  innotiiit  quia  pregnans  est . 
Quam  cum  pregnantem  ac  Christi  nomen  invocantem  Rex  pater  ejus  agnovisset, 
secundum  patrie  legem,  quasi  filiam  fornicariam  legisque  paterne  prevaricatricem 
lapidibus  obrui  jussit  .  Legis  enim  decretum  eorum  tempore  illo  jubebat  ut  omnis 
mulier  ex  nobilibus  orta  natalibus  si  deprehensa  fuisset  in  fornicatione,  lapidum 
ictibus  obrueretur ;  ancilla  vero,  signo  nequicie  ia  facie  sua  expresso,  in  obprobrium 
omnibus  haberetur  . 

Quomodo  Uberata  sit  a  morte  in  montis  cimia. 

Tradita  ergo  spiculatoribus  pregnante  juvencula  contentio  orta  est  inter  eos,  quis 
primum  in  illam  jaceret  lapillum  .  Sed  quia  nullus  exactorum  in  regiam  prolem 
*  lapidem  mittere  presumebat,  et  decretum  judicium  si  licet  in  dictum  vocari  nequa- 
quam  audebant  pretermittere,  ducta  est  in  supercilio  montis  qui  Kepduf  vocatur,  ut 
iu  biga  posita  ab  ipsius  cacumine  precipitata  dire  neci  commendaretur,  et  sic  exactores 
quasi  innoxii  mortis  ejus  viderentur  .  Cumque  in  mortis  periculo  jam  esset  constituta, 
ejus  causam  infortunii  divino  agnoscens  spiramine,  direetis  oculis  et  manibus  in  celum
